    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }

      AppleID.auth.init({

<span class="expandableContainer"><span class="truncatedContent">I think that the faith is significant in the book, not the atheism. The faith and only the faith wins after all. And I think that even if there is any<a class="jsShow showAction" href="#">…more</a></span><span class="fullContent">I think that the faith is significant in the book, not the atheism. The faith and only the faith wins after all. And I think that even if there is any atheism, that is also some kind of faith. Of faith that there isn't God.<a class="jsHide hideAction" href="#">(less)</a></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer35088891" class="readable"

<span id="freeTextContainer8784622909487025843">Ο Χριστός ξανασταυρώνεται = Christ Recrucified = Le Christ recrucifie, Nikos Kazantzakis <br/><br/>The story concerns the attempts of a Greek village community to stage a Passion Play - which, as the title suggests, ends up with their in effect re-enacting the events of Jesus Christ's trial, suffering and death. It takes place in a Greek village, Lycovrisi (Wolf-spring), under Ottoman rule.<br/><br/>The village holds Passion Plays every seven years and the elders of the village choose the actors from among the vill</span>
  <span id="freeText8784622909487025843" style="display:none">Ο Χριστός ξανασταυρώνεται = Christ Recrucified = Le Christ recrucifie, Nikos Kazantzakis <br /><br />The story concerns the attempts of a Greek village community to stage a Passion Play - which, as the title suggests, ends up with their in effect re-enacting the events of Jesus Christ's trial, suffering and death. It takes place in a Greek village, Lycovrisi (Wolf-spring), under Ottoman rule.<br /><br />The village holds Passion Plays every seven years and the elders of the village choose the actors from among the villagers. Manolios, who is chosen to play the role of Christ, is a humble shepherd boy who was once a novice in a monastery. <br /><br />Yannakkos becomes Apostle Peter. He is a merchant-peddler who travels with his donkey through the villages and sells his items. He is warm-hearted, naïve and loves his donkey above all else. <br /><br />Michelis, the son of the wealthy nobleman old Patriarcheas, becomes Apostle John. Kostandis, the owner of the village café, is Apostle James the Great. He is good-hearted, willing to share, but confused. Then comes Panayotaros, who is chosen to be Judas. <br /><br />He is a wild, passionate man, waiting for revenge. The widow Katerina is Mary Magdalene. She is the village's prostitute. She is beautiful, but of course an outsider in the village, not caring about anybody's opinion. But she is the most generous one and in the end gives her life for what she believes in.<br /><br />Then the Elders of Lycovrissi are introduced. There is the Priest Grigoris — a domineering man who bends God's will to his own. Archon Patriarcheas is the leader of the village. He only lives for his own pleasure. Old Ladas is a miser who is obsessed with his money but lives in poverty so that he doesn't have to spend any of it. Hadji Nikolis is the schoolmaster, who means well but is ineffectual, haunted by fear of his brother the priest.<br /><br />The whole story is made colorful by the Turkish household consisting of The Agha, the Lord of Lycovrissi. He lives surrounded by his Oriental splendor, drinks himself crazy and enjoys rakı and pretty boys. Hussein is the guard, a giant Oriental who does everything his master asks of him.<br /><br />تاریخ نخستین خوانش: در ماه نوامبر سال 2005 میلادی<br /><br />عنوان: مسیح باز مصلوب؛ نویسنده: نیکوس کازانتزاکیس؛ مترجم: محمد قاضی؛ تهران، خوارزمی، 1349؛ در 645ص؛ چاپ هشتم 1385؛ شابک 9644870298؛ در 634ص؛ موضوع داستانهای نویسندگان یونانی - سده 20م<br /><br />عنوان: مسیح باز مصلوب؛ نویسنده: نیکوس کازانتزاکیس؛ مترجم محمود سلطانیه؛ تهران، جامی، 1378؛ در 578ص؛ شابک 9645620694؛ چاپ سوم 1380؛<br /><br />هرچه از این نویسنده خوانده ام، مدهوشم کرده، «زوربای یونانی» را بارها نوشیده ام، و اکنون در این نامه، مسیح بار دیگر باز هم مصلوب شده است؛ محل رخداد داستان، یکی از روستاهای یونان است، که توسط «آقا»، حکمران منصوب از طرف دولت «عثمانی»، اداره می‌شود.؛ «مانولیوس» نام شخصیت اصلی این رمان است، که الگوی خود را مسیح قرار داده‌، و داستان کلی رمان، روای ِ داستان زندگی وی، و تلاش‌ها و کوشش‌هایی ست، که او برای یاری به مردمان می‌کند.؛ «آقا»، اختیاردار جان و مال و ناموس اهالی آبادی ست؛ ضمن این‌که در روابط خصوصی آن‌ها دخالتی ندارد.؛ اختلاف بین «آقا» و ساکنان، زمانی آغاز می‌شود، که «یوسفک»، پسربچه ی امردی، که «آقا» از «ترکیه» آورده‌، و مونس ساعات خوش اوست، به قتل می‌رسد.؛ «کازانتزاکیس» در این رمان، با موشکافی، تضاد بین حاکم «ترک»، و رعایای «یونانی» را، با طنزی دلنشین به تصویر کشیده، و از سویی دیگر، اتحاد این دو را، برای حفظ منافع مشترک، و سرکوب جنبش نوخاسته‌ ای، که به مذاق متعصبین کلیسا نیز ناخوش‌آیند است، نشان می‌دهد.؛ این کتاب تصویری گویا از عناصر موجود در جامعه، و نماینده ی بینش‌های گوناگونی است، که با عنوانهای «ناسیونالیسم»، «سوسیالیسم»، «خرده بورژوازی»، «سرمایه‌ داری»، «فئودالیسم» و «انقلاب»، در جایگاه مخصوص به خود، اعلام موجودیت می‌کنند<br /><br />تاریخ بهنگام رسانی 25/05/1399هجری خورشیدی؛ ا. شربیانی</span>

<span id="freeTextContainer17110408279077227869">I read this book while in college, more than a decade ago, and I don't remember the details of the plot. I do remember though how much I loved it and how much it affected me. I remember sobbing at 4 am at night while reading Manolios's confession, feeling the suffering he was going through.   <span id="freeText17110408279077227869" style="display:none">I read this book while in college, more than a decade ago, and I don't remember the details of the plot. I do remember though how much I loved it and how much it affected me. I remember sobbing at 4 am at night while reading Manolios's confession, feeling the suffering he was going through. It is definitely one of my favorite books of all times.<br /><br />I won't do the story justice right now, but if I had to summarize it the way I think of it, I would say it's about the struggle of the man, every man to become a better person and fight his selfishness, his darker self. It's about the struggle against your personal interest and comfort in order to provide help to fellow humans that are in need. It's about being able to feel compassion. It's about reminding us how easy it is to shut our eyes, turn our heads away and allow for tragic events to happen.</span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer86688768" class="readable"

<span id="freeTextContainer17331329020280711965">Nikos Kazantzakis (1883-1957) was surely the greatest Greek author of the 20th century. His writing is passionate, colorful, down to earth. He almost won the Nobel Prize for Literature in 1947, losing to Camus by one vote. He's most famous in this country for Zorba the Greek (made into a popular movie with Anthony Quinn), & also The Last Temptation of Christ (made into a controversial movie directed by Scorsese**). I read his books when I was in high school, & this one, The Greek Passion, was my</span>
  <span id="freeText17331329020280711965" style="display:none">Nikos Kazantzakis (1883-1957) was surely the greatest Greek author of the 20th century. His writing is passionate, colorful, down to earth. He almost won the Nobel Prize for Literature in 1947, losing to Camus by one vote. He's most famous in this country for Zorba the Greek (made into a popular movie with Anthony Quinn), &amp; also The Last Temptation of Christ (made into a controversial movie directed by Scorsese**). I read his books when I was in high school, &amp; this one, The Greek Passion, was my favorite. Re-reading it now, I probably appreciated his use of language more than I did as a teenager. A vivid portrayal of village life in the early 20th century, when Greek was still under Turkish control, &amp; of an intense Christianity, in which all suffering is good because it purifies us!<br /><br />-----<br />*The book &amp; movie were both controversial, because the story portrayed Christ as living a real human life. Kazantzakis was excommunicated by the Greek Orthodox Church, &amp; the Roman Catholic Church put this book on their Index of Forbidden Books.</span>
  <a data-text-id="17331329020280711965" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_2684774322" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2684774322" />
    <a title="Marc" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2876105-marc"><img alt="Marc" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1569160429p2/2876105.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2684774322?book_show_action=true">Jan 30, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Marc" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Marc" href="/user/show/2876105-marc">Marc</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780571190218" href="/book/show/159273.Christ_Recrucified">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2876105-marc?shelf=greek-literature">greek-literature</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2684774322" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17391693671555669212">Almost-Nobel laureate Kazantzakis is best known for "Zorba the Greek" and since the film of Scorsese also for "The Last Temptation of Christ". This book, "Christ recrucified" is a little less known, and perhaps it is also qualitatively a lesser book. In this novel the style of Kazantzakis is very 19th century-like: the rural setting (a small Greek village in Turkish territory), the exaggerated dramatics of the main characters, the detailed description of their internal soul stirrings, and finall</span>
  <span id="freeText17391693671555669212" style="display:none">Almost-Nobel laureate Kazantzakis is best known for "Zorba the Greek" and since the film of Scorsese also for "The Last Temptation of Christ". This book, "Christ recrucified" is a little less known, and perhaps it is also qualitatively a lesser book. In this novel the style of Kazantzakis is very 19th century-like: the rural setting (a small Greek village in Turkish territory), the exaggerated dramatics of the main characters, the detailed description of their internal soul stirrings, and finally the very clear moral undertone (the good and the bad are clearly distinct from each other); you would not expect all of that in a novel written after the Second World War.<br /><br />Reportedly, Kazantzakis was very affected by the civil war in his country, Greece 1946-1949, and that is very clear in this story. Among other things, he shows how the communist threat is used falsely by the leaders of the village (economic, political and, above all, ecclesiastical) to protect their privileges and interests. Especially in the second half of the book the fear of the so-called 'Bolsheviks' drives the villagers to frenzied violence, incited by their very hypocritical orthodox priest.<br /><br />The novel seduces by the large and small human dramas that are presented in a touching way, by the fervent sense of justice by which the 'good' characters are driven and their struggle to make the right life choices, and by the lyrical-epic character of the narrative. But equally the story repulses by the exaggerated dramatic, the caricatural moralization, and the all too showy parallel with the Christian passion story. I had expected more of this.</span>
  <a data-text-id="17391693671555669212" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_902665877" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/902665877" />
    <a title="Matt Ely" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/19071212-matt-ely"><img alt="Matt Ely" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1365063487p2/19071212.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/902665877?book_show_action=true">Apr 06, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Matt Ely" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Matt Ely" href="/user/show/19071212-matt-ely">Matt Ely</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/19071212-matt-ely?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/19071212-matt-ely?shelf=theology">theology</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer902665877" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8128555228314062358">The author's style seems, to me, inseperable from that of Dostoyevsky. The difference, however, is that while Dostoyevsky's works usually traverse from darkness to a modicum of light and redemption, Kazantzakis' book starts in the light and shows that when light is fully pursued, it often leads to darkness. The theme, as I see it, is one of conversion. A few characters are compelled, almost by forces outside themselves, to take a fresh look at the life of Jesus and to take it seriously. They qui</span>
  <span id="freeText8128555228314062358" style="display:none">The author's style seems, to me, inseperable from that of Dostoyevsky. The difference, however, is that while Dostoyevsky's works usually traverse from darkness to a modicum of light and redemption, Kazantzakis' book starts in the light and shows that when light is fully pursued, it often leads to darkness. The theme, as I see it, is one of conversion. A few characters are compelled, almost by forces outside themselves, to take a fresh look at the life of Jesus and to take it seriously. They quickly find, however, that a life honestly lived in pursuit of the the Gospel makes them enemies of their families, the state, and even the church. Kazantzakis is not so naive that he makes the convert's experience a universal good; it is tempered by implicit warnings that constant spiritual reawakening can lead to destruction as well. But the brunt of his criticism clearly lies with rich individuals and institutions that follow the Gospel of a homeless man, Jesus. It is a deep, thought out picture of hypocrisy that certainly hasn't lost relevance for its age. </span>
  <a data-text-id="8128555228314062358" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2469142860" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2469142860" />
    <a title="Ami" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/8502789-ami"><img alt="Ami" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1605127295p2/8502789.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2469142860?book_show_action=true">Jul 24, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ami" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ami" href="/user/show/8502789-ami">Ami</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/8502789-ami?shelf=religion-theology">religion-theology</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2469142860" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8487212140018945924">I struggle to find the proper words to describe what reading this book did to me. It was almost a spiritual experience. It moved me to tears, made me question all my ideas about religion, its place in society, morality, justice... Reading Kazantzakis proved to be much more uplifting than reading the Bible or other sacrimonious canonical texts, because belief in Kazantzakis is something real, raw, human. He adds to belief that element of eternal struggle that the Church would not admit, but we al</span>
  <span id="freeText8487212140018945924" style="display:none">I struggle to find the proper words to describe what reading this book did to me. It was almost a spiritual experience. It moved me to tears, made me question all my ideas about religion, its place in society, morality, justice... Reading Kazantzakis proved to be much more uplifting than reading the Bible or other sacrimonious canonical texts, because belief in Kazantzakis is something real, raw, human. He adds to belief that element of eternal struggle that the Church would not admit, but we all feel: the more we want to believe, the harder it gets, especially when all the evidence seems to point in the opposite direction. <br /><br />This was the implicit message of the novel, for me. In the last pages, the refugee priest Fotis has a dream of himself chasing a holy golden bird for millenia, never reaching it, never giving up. That is all of us, trying to chase God, trying to make him come alive, but never quite managing. <br /><br />The explicit message was a warning about the dire consequences of what happens when you almost succeed in bringing God back into society: it breaks down. Personally I always thought that the actual Christian teachings are revolutionary. They were born of a society under duress, who had to put up with both a foreign invander and a corrupt, complicit elite. They are violently anti-capitalist, almost communist (in fact the characters that were attempting to turn faith into action in the novel were accussed of being Bolsheviks). They ask people to go against all their most deep-rooted instincts, including self-preservation, romantic love or familial attachment. The most important Christian values were always faith and charity: enough faith to follow God's law without questioning it, and enough charity to give everything you have to those in need. Try to apply these rigorously to any human society and watch the chaos unfold. People will be angry to the point of hysteria, they will call you crazy, they will hate you and do whatever it takes to silence you, possibly forever. <br /><br />And this is essentially what happened in Lycovrissi, the Greek village in Kazantzakis' novel. A group of people are chosen to represent Christ, the Apostles Peter, John, James and Judas, and Mary Magdalene for a Passion Play that takes place in the village every 7 years. With the exception of Manolios, the would-be Christ, none of them take this very seriously, but the roles end up coming alive and overtaking their lives. They struggle with faith, with love (love of God vs. romantic/familial love), and with temptation, and come out of it not only as better people, but also as revolutionaries. When a group of refugees comes to their village, only to be driven out in disgust by the village elders, the would-be Christ and his band strive to help them. This brings them to an open conflict with the village elders, a conflict that will eventually end in blood being spilled. In this conflict, some unassigned roles of the Passion Play make themselves obvious: the local priest Grigoris plays the role of the establishment that killed Jesus, and the Turkish overlord, the Agha, becomes Pontius Pilate. What is incredible is that as they descend further and further into chaos, none of them seem to realize that they really brought the Bible to life. <br /><br /><a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[This differs from the Bible in one key aspect, however: the Resurrection, the ultimate proof of God's existence. There is no Resurrection here, no miracle (although a few apparent miracles do happen in the novel beforehand). We see no proof that faith has served Manolios and his band in any way, as they all finish the novel either dead or much worse off than they were at the start. The novel builds up an incredible amount of religious enthusiasm, only to frustrate it at the end. It's like Kazantzakis asks whether we believe humans can find God in their lifetimes?, and appears to be answering 'yes', before he closes the novel in our face with a resounding 'no'. There's a bittersweet quality to it, a philosophical resignation: this is what it means to be human. To believe, to raise yourself to impossible heights on that belief, even, but to never be sure, to never have proof. Chasing God remains, now and forever, our curse. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span></span>
  <a data-text-id="8487212140018945924" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <a title="Jane" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/7177008-jane"><img alt="Jane" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1388366904p2/7177008.jpg" /></a>

<div id="review_1637090004" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1637090004" />
    <a title="Beatrice Apetrei" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/22548668-beatrice-apetrei"><img alt="Beatrice Apetrei" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1586449703p2/22548668.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1637090004?book_show_action=true">May 13, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Beatrice Apetrei" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Beatrice Apetrei" href="/user/show/22548668-beatrice-apetrei">Beatrice Apetrei</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/22548668-beatrice-apetrei?shelf=classics">classics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/22548668-beatrice-apetrei?shelf=own">own</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/22548668-beatrice-apetrei?shelf=religion">religion</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1637090004" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5887885587317972119"><blockquote>"There is nothing to writing. All you do is sit down at a typewriter and bleed." Ernest Hemingway</blockquote><br/><p>This is more than just fiction, is more than just a story, but then again, all books are more than just stories , some action in which you can find delight or not; Christ Recrucified felt more like an exploration of human capabilities to reach the highest they can get.</p><br/><p> It starts as a game, or rather a play. People from a Greek village, Lycovrisi, are chosen by those who held the political, religious </p></span>
  <span id="freeText5887885587317972119" style="display:none"><blockquote>"There is nothing to writing. All you do is sit down at a typewriter and bleed." Ernest Hemingway</blockquote><br /><p>This is more than just fiction, is more than just a story, but then again, all books are more than just stories , some action in which you can find delight or not; Christ Recrucified felt more like an exploration of human capabilities to reach the highest they can get.</p><br /><p> It starts as a game, or rather a play. People from a Greek village, Lycovrisi, are chosen by those who held the political, religious and economical power, according to their actions and personalities, to re-act the events of Jesus Christ's trial : Yannakkos ( Apostle Peter ), Michelis ( Apostle John ), Kostandis ( Apostle James the Great ), Panayotaros ( Judas ), Katerina (Mary Magdalene ), and lastly Manolios ( Jesus Christ ). It's beautiful how at the beginning they don't think of themselves as being worthy of / suitable for the roles , but as days pass, they become more and more who they are supposed to impersonate.</p><br /><p><b>The writing </b>: It is exquisite. With no exaggeration. Kazantzakis is a talented author. The phrases are not too short, the dialogue is not hollow ( I don't think there were any shallow remarks or dull conversations ) and the paragraphs give a lot of information regarding : the weather, the places , the mentality that governed those times, the ideas, the vices of men, the people and their beastly instincts, how they separate from animals, how they fight against what threatens them and their beliefs. Everything comes to life and draws you in. One minute you are Manolios, in his attempts to sacrifice his nothingness to God, the other you are Yannakkos, a merchant-peddler talking with his donkey ( his only treasure ). Then you get some bits from Panayotaros and his loathing of the elders and the village for forcing him into becoming Judas. But wait, you are once more determined Michelis, trying to cut lose the ropes that tie him to Earth and so empty himself of anything that could turn into vices, so he can fill up with Light. </p><br /><p><br />You can empathize with all <b>characters</b> : you understand their reasons because you understand their way of thinking, Kazantzakis really gives you a glimpse of what expectations or mentality certain people have according to the environment they grew up in. Love them, hate them, laugh at them etc, they exist and you know they do, because they are <i> real </i> . They are believable and surely you've met one or another. You cannot classify them in good or bad : there is no black or white, but shades of grey, lighter or darker. <br /></p><br /><p><br />At first, I read books out of curiosity, of desire for living in an unreachable world, narrowed between two hard covers and showed in black letters on a white paper, of longing for a different life lived through different eyes. Then, when I grew, I started looking for understanding, not necessarily an author's understanding of me, but rather the exact opposite. I want to know how, why and for what he is writing : what is he trying to prove, what does he feel, what is he looking for, <i><b>what is he feeling</b></i>. When I first started reading one of Kazantzakis' books, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/8737.The_Last_Temptation_of_Christ" title="The Last Temptation of Christ by Nikos Kazantzakis" rel="nofollow noopener">The Last Temptation of Christ</a> , I knew what I would find : a man trying to find the Truth, or perhaps, trying to understand a small part of what he thought the Truth is. I will not tell you if he achieved his purpose, or if he managed to heal his wounds ( because we know that art is a way of healing or of emptying oneself of unbearable emotions ), because not even after finishing it I could not answer it . I think he did not expect the same people to read more than one of his books. Because shortly after, I read <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/82531.Christ_Recrucified" title="Christ Recrucified by Nikos Kazantzakis" rel="nofollow noopener">Christ Recrucified</a> ( which had been written a few years <i> before </i>) and I felt as if I was reading the same book once more. <i> Same questions, same doubts, same answers </i> . Or better said : <b>same lack of answers</b>. </p><br /><p><br />Now you might ask "why would he write such a book?". In my opinion he tried to <i>understand</i> how far can a man go in emptying his mind of himself and filling it up with Light, with understanding of God, with comprehending Him.It's unimaginable what boundaries should break for him to reach the sky. The question is can he still be a man afterwards? Nikos Kazantzakis came with an answer ( answer that is found in more than one book, so maybe he was unsure ? or perhaps he simply wanted to convince himself of its <i>Truth</i>?).</p><br /><p>I wonder what's <b>your answer</b>.</p></span>
  <a data-text-id="5887885587317972119" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_201488285" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/201488285" />
    <a title="Howard" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5928275-howard"><img alt="Howard" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/m_50x66-82093808bca726cb3249a493fbd3bd0f.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/201488285?book_show_action=true">Aug 23, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Howard" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Howard" href="/user/show/5928275-howard">Howard</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5928275-howard?shelf=europe">europe</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5928275-howard?shelf=my-top-20">my-top-20</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer201488285" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13972796800002044579">This is a remarkably clever, emotional and challenging novel. It is in essence a simple story: a Greek village re-enacts the life of Christ every four years, individuals are given parts to play - how much are they inherently like or do they taken on themselves the biblical/personalities roles? The long novel of 470pages deepens the idea on so many levels you'll love it. <br/><br/>Ok a little more detail. The location is a smallish town called Lycovrissi on mainland Turkey (Anatolia) occupied by Greeks in </span>
  <span id="freeText13972796800002044579" style="display:none">This is a remarkably clever, emotional and challenging novel. It is in essence a simple story: a Greek village re-enacts the life of Christ every four years, individuals are given parts to play - how much are they inherently like or do they taken on themselves the biblical/personalities roles? The long novel of 470pages deepens the idea on so many levels you'll love it. <br /><br />Ok a little more detail. The location is a smallish town called Lycovrissi on mainland Turkey (Anatolia) occupied by Greeks in the 1920s. The town is overseen by the Turkish Agha, who is just about the only Turk mentioned (except his two teenage male lovers?); though the town itself is run by the rich miser Ladas, the old Dr Patrarchis and the conniving priest Grigoris. <br /><br />The town is host to the usual variety of individuals and I found it useful to keep a note of the characters and their given roles (by the priest Grigoris) in the re-enactment so here goes: <br />Panayataros - saddler (Judas) <br />Kostandis - family man and cafe owner (James) <br />Yanakos - carrier and mule owner (Peter) <br />Katerina - widow and hussy (Magdelen) <br />Patrachis - doctor and heavy weight (Pilate) <br />Michelis - Partachis's son (John) <br />Ladas - married to Martha (Caiaphus) <br />Manolios - sheep herd (Jesus) <br />Mariori - Grigoris daughter <br />Hadis - schoolmaster &amp; Grigoris brother <br />Leno - Manolios' love <br />Fortunas - captain and Agha's drinking buddy <br />Demitri - butcher <br /><br />The village is going strong until a group of displaced (from the Smyrna district), poor and starving Greeks headed by father Fotis descend on the town and moves into the local mountain caves (at a sign from God). The righteous want to help (e.g. Manolios) while the wealthy self-interested want rid (Ladas and Grigoris). The depth of the story comes from the roles the individual have taken on, the turn of events they themselves seem to initiate to actually self-fulfill them e.g. Manolios, and the deeper true roles they actually play e.g. is it Patrachis or the Agha who actual behave like Pilate? Sufficient to say it all ends in tears not least helped along up by Panayatros who introduces the idea of a Bolshevik conspiracy. <br /><br />I loved this story and think it Kazantzakis's best. My blood boiled at the inequity of the priest and the town. The characters are deep and well rounded. There are lots of side interests Mariori's illness, Agha's passions, murder, the hypocrisy, Magdelen's righteousness, the poor's need for violence, Yanakos love of his mule and so on. The one thing I suspect went over my head was the necessary knowledge of the less well known (to me) biblical characters (James, Peter etc) but despite this lack of Christian understanding I can recommend the book.</span>
  <a data-text-id="13972796800002044579" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

The high virtues suffer an almost total defeat against the powers of greed, social status, jealousy and hate. <br/><br/>What is to be lerned from this book? perhaps that no nation, religious affilia</span>
  <span id="freeText11025239974136550433" style="display:none">Christ is crucified once more and after 2000 years mankind is still embrioled in a seemingly endless cycle of tragedy. In a small greek community in Anatolia, the almost superhuman virtues of greek ancient ancestry and orthodox christian faith is put to the test against basic human weaknesses and inclinations. The high virtues suffer an almost total defeat against the powers of greed, social status, jealousy and hate. <br /><br />What is to be lerned from this book? perhaps that no nation, religious affiliation or ancestral descent, embodies the people it is supposed to encompass with a higher morality in practice. We are all sinners basically! </span>

I finished reading this book soon after visiting the tomb of Nikos Kazantzakis in Heraklion on the island of Crete. The headstone reads as follows: Δεν ελπίζω τίποτε. Δεν φοβʊμαι τίποτε. Είμαι λεύτερος. (I hope for nothing. I fear nothing. I am free.)</span>

              <span id="reviewTextContainer3559674674" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17949497792182186897">"What a thing the world is, in spite of everything!"<br/><br/>This is a novel about a bunch of ordinary people struggling against poverty and oppression who are then proclaimed socialists by their so-called 'betters' and roundly persecuted.<br/>It may sound like a familiar theme.<br/>Kazzie's novel, though, takes place in the waning years of the Ottoman Empire in a little Greek village ruled by a Turkish official who is more interested in his boyfriend than the affairs of the village. He leaves things to the local </span>
  <span id="freeText17949497792182186897" style="display:none">"What a thing the world is, in spite of everything!"<br /><br />This is a novel about a bunch of ordinary people struggling against poverty and oppression who are then proclaimed socialists by their so-called 'betters' and roundly persecuted.<br />It may sound like a familiar theme.<br />Kazzie's novel, though, takes place in the waning years of the Ottoman Empire in a little Greek village ruled by a Turkish official who is more interested in his boyfriend than the affairs of the village. He leaves things to the local "notables", the head priest and various other local assholes. When the survivors of a Turkish purge of a Greek village show up as refugees, though, in rags and starving, the village betters refuse to help them, often violently, certainly vociferously.<br />The backdrop for all this horrible shit is the three young men chosen to act in next Easter's Passion Play, as Christ and two disciples. As the novel progresses, these three guys, absorbed in their roles, begin to delve deeply into Christ's compassionate message and become the champions of the starving refugees.<br />As you can imagine, shit hits the fan quickly and bloodily, but the novel's expanse is a beautiful tale of how ordinary people can interpret the Gospel, or what-have-you, in personal ways for the common good. Sadly, it is also about how fucking awful people can be to one another. Nevertheless, suffering is a good tool for life.<br />In the same way that their roles inspired them to try and actively portray those they are to perform as, so fiction at times can be a powerful engine for emulation, if anyone would ever bother. But I guess too few, like the antagonists in <i>Christ</i>, realize that what life is for some is often guaranteed by the good will of others, for the common good and the example of the common good. Unfortunately for us, it often doesn't end well.</span>
  <a data-text-id="17949497792182186897" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer6594446348152998482">I started reading this book knowing that it would be great because of the previous book “Zorba the Greek” for the same author. Which is by the way a great book<br/>The book is about a Christian community. Starts with preparation for a play to re-act the crucification of Christ. The flow of the book opens a variety of philosophical questions such as good and evil, talking in the name of God, misusing religious system for personal benefit, manipulating people by the misuse religious talk, rebellious yo</span>
  <span id="freeText6594446348152998482" style="display:none">I started reading this book knowing that it would be great because of the previous book “Zorba the Greek” for the same author. Which is by the way a great book<br />The book is about a Christian community. Starts with preparation for a play to re-act the crucification of Christ. The flow of the book opens a variety of philosophical questions such as good and evil, talking in the name of God, misusing religious system for personal benefit, manipulating people by the misuse religious talk, rebellious youth standing up against high figures on the community, etc. It raises all these questions in such a lay language. <br />The book is pretty old and it’s a big mistake to try to interpret by the use of today’s knowledge. <br />The author reveals that the evil ends up winning over the good because of the principle of sacrificing to save others and act nicely even for people who harm you. Which is where I would disagree. And also one reason for my rating.<br /><br />Even though the book is in a Christian context, it can be easily understood by anyone who has zero knowledge in Christianity. I recommend it for those who may wonder how religious guidance be misused. <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="6594446348152998482" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/110053405" />
    <a title="Ensiform" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3843117-ensiform"><img alt="Ensiform" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1275973241p2/3843117.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/110053405?book_show_action=true">Jul 04, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ensiform" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ensiform" href="/user/show/3843117-ensiform">Ensiform</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780671212162" href="/book/show/254828.The_Greek_Passion">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3843117-ensiform?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3843117-ensiform?shelf=greek">greek</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer110053405" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14022199390894134723">An allegory for the death of Christ. Very Kazantzakian - full of pious sinners, unrepentant whores, sex, violence (two priests wrestle, very graphically, at one point), terrifying divine revelations, and so on. I liked it a lot. The Agha (a.k.a. the Pilate) was a great character. Again, Kazantzakis stresses the variety of paths that God stretches before us --- martyrdom, asceticism, domestic comfort –-- all, perhaps, equally valid.</span>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_871513920" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/871513920" />
    <a title="Peggy Haymes" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/10713901-peggy-haymes"><img alt="Peggy Haymes" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1394406835p2/10713901.jpg" /></a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer871513920" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8027845971041715073">Every seven years in a Greek village, townspeople are elected to play the parts in the presentation of the Passion story. Once assigned their parts, they are charged with living as this character for the year leading up to Holy Week.<br/><br/>The task become more than just an intellectual exercise as they townspeople wrestle with that it means to take the Gospels seriously and deeply.<br/><br/>I first read this book many years ago and have just returned to it. It is exquisite.</span>

          </span>
        </div>

Amazingly, real situations in the village put each of them in a analogous real-life role so that they are living the parts they had been chosen to play. <div id="review_2679460036" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2679460036" />
    <a title="Thomas Guidotti" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/71643871-thomas-guidotti"><img alt="Thomas Guidotti" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1556234515p2/71643871.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2679460036?book_show_action=true">Jun 04, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Thomas Guidotti" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Thomas Guidotti" href="/user/show/71643871-thomas-guidotti">Thomas Guidotti</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback " href="/book/show/39698911-christ-recrucified">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2679460036" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10706429277863755735">This marks the end of my journey with Kazantzakis's main works, at least for now. The central themes of Kaznatzakis's thought are very present here, namely the conflict between flesh and spirit and the idea that the path of men is the one that rises. Manolios's transformation from a lowly shepherd to a mirror for Christ is very captivating. The inner turmoil that he faces as he struggles to attain the Christian ideal is universal to all those who suffer. The characters of Michelis and Pope Fotis</span>
  <span id="freeText10706429277863755735" style="display:none">This marks the end of my journey with Kazantzakis's main works, at least for now. The central themes of Kaznatzakis's thought are very present here, namely the conflict between flesh and spirit and the idea that the path of men is the one that rises. Manolios's transformation from a lowly shepherd to a mirror for Christ is very captivating. The inner turmoil that he faces as he struggles to attain the Christian ideal is universal to all those who suffer. The characters of Michelis and Pope Fotis are also very interesting. Michelis's journey from a young archon to an ascetic works nicely in parallel with Manolios, and Pope Fotis lends a lot to the story. <br /><br />I don't think that one has to identify with Christianity to enjoy this work. Kazantzakis uses the gospels in the way many other authors use Greek mythology - as a way to identify their story with stories familiar to us all. </span>
  <a data-text-id="10706429277863755735" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer5433423095440436094">A book that may change your view of humanity. This is one of the best books I have read. Although in essence, it re-enacts Christ's passion the depth of this book is so profound that it throws up, through its extremely strong characters, our failures and our strengths as a human race. Set in the early part of the 20th century in a part of Greece still under Ottoman rule, the scenery is vivid, the scenes themselves recognisably Greek in their portrayal. The author travelled widely and the reader </span>
  <span id="freeText5433423095440436094" style="display:none">A book that may change your view of humanity. This is one of the best books I have read. Although in essence, it re-enacts Christ's passion the depth of this book is so profound that it throws up, through its extremely strong characters, our failures and our strengths as a human race. Set in the early part of the 20th century in a part of Greece still under Ottoman rule, the scenery is vivid, the scenes themselves recognisably Greek in their portrayal. The author travelled widely and the reader can feel that perhaps he doesn't share the same blind faith of good and bad as his characters.</span>

<div id="review_115612845" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/115612845" />
    <a title="Ted Dettweiler" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1297419-ted-dettweiler"><img alt="Ted Dettweiler" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1268006918p2/1297419.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/115612845?book_show_action=true">Aug 07, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ted Dettweiler" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ted Dettweiler" href="/user/show/1297419-ted-dettweiler">Ted Dettweiler</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
                  <em>
                    This review has been hidden because it contains spoilers. To view it,
                    <a href="#" onclick="Effect.toggle(&#39;reviewTextContainer115612845&#39;); return false;">click here.</a>
                  </em>
              <span id="reviewTextContainer115612845" class="readable"
                    style="display:none"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13987877950415090996">This book is titled "Christ Recrucified" in some editions. I prefer the title on my book "The Greek Passion". I probably would not have even considered reading the book under the former title - doesn't sound like a novel - sounds too much like theology.<br/><br/>This prolific Greek writer (no longer living) has more than one book on the "passion of Christ". This one is very good.<br/><br/>Manolos, the shepherd, chosen to play Christ, is a sympathetic character. It is very difficult to portray the life of Christ in </span>
  <span id="freeText13987877950415090996" style="display:none">This book is titled "Christ Recrucified" in some editions. I prefer the title on my book "The Greek Passion". I probably would not have even considered reading the book under the former title - doesn't sound like a novel - sounds too much like theology.<br /><br />This prolific Greek writer (no longer living) has more than one book on the "passion of Christ". This one is very good.<br /><br />Manolos, the shepherd, chosen to play Christ, is a sympathetic character. It is very difficult to portray the life of Christ in writing, so this method of writing of a person who takes on the role of Christ offers more freedom.<br /><br />When we read about Christ in the gospels he comes across as apolitical - though he was crucified for political reasons. Manolos takes up politics, since there is no need for him to accomplish what Christ did once for all, and it leads to his death. Like Christ, Manolos dies for political reasons. This is a "Greek" passion, though, so it is at the hands of the Greek Orthodox church that Manolos dies. This book has no problem portraying this church of being capable of doing the same to Manolos as the Jewish "church" did to Christ. Fortunately there is a sympathetic Orthodox priest in this story as well so the Greek Orthodox faith is not all painted with one wide brush.<br /><br />This book made me want to continue to read Kazantzakis' writing as he definitely has something to say. By Goodreaders comments and quotes I'll probably look for "Zorba The Greek" next and then "The Last Temptation of Christ" to see if the movie version totally ruined what Kazantzakis wanted to say.<br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="13987877950415090996" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <a title="Olethros" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/7962096-olethros"><img alt="Olethros" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/u_50x66-632230dc9882b4352d753eedf9396530.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/432269317?book_show_action=true">Oct 11, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Olethros" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Olethros" href="/user/show/7962096-olethros">Olethros</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Leather Bound 9789607948229" href="/book/show/6616654">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer432269317" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17629217112740467338">The first thing that strikes you when you read this book is the language and setting. Written more than 50 years ago, and describing events taking place nearly another 50 years before that, it at times feels like a fantasy setting. I suppose this will be doubly the case for those who have had little contact with Christian imagery and traditions.<br/><br/>The book's title basically summarises the plot, and you can guess its broad outlines and the role of each character after the first few pages. What makes</span>
  <span id="freeText17629217112740467338" style="display:none">The first thing that strikes you when you read this book is the language and setting. Written more than 50 years ago, and describing events taking place nearly another 50 years before that, it at times feels like a fantasy setting. I suppose this will be doubly the case for those who have had little contact with Christian imagery and traditions.<br /><br />The book's title basically summarises the plot, and you can guess its broad outlines and the role of each character after the first few pages. What makes the book interesting is the description and transformation of characters, the depiction of life in that era and region, the social dynamics, and the theme of new immigrants as outcasts and undesirables, which is particularly relevant these days.<br /><br />The Christian themes maybe a bit too much for some people, but they certainly didn't bother me that much, even when people go into extended monologues. They are juxtaposed with the everyday brutality of life in a small agricultural community, which is a great contrast. I certainly didn't expect to like the book so much, but it is flawlessly executed.<br /><br />I have read this in the original Greek, and have no idea what the quality of translations is.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="17629217112740467338" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/117842927?book_show_action=true">Aug 20, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781412812610" href="/book/show/6832317-the-greek-passion">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/937148-mary?shelf=already-read-fiction">already-read-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer117842927" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14465161151565698131">This is beautifully written book with a very mixed message. The story begins with a fairly prosperous Greek village selecting the players for the next year's Passion play. As the year goes by Manolios, chosen as the Christ, becomes more Christ-like, while the Judas becomes a dangerous antagonist to not only Manolios, but to all who might be influenced to follow him in selfless devotion to the poor, specifically the refugees from a nearby village which has been pillaged by the Turks. The inevitab</span>
  <span id="freeText14465161151565698131" style="display:none">This is beautifully written book with a very mixed message. The story begins with a fairly prosperous Greek village selecting the players for the next year's Passion play. As the year goes by Manolios, chosen as the Christ, becomes more Christ-like, while the Judas becomes a dangerous antagonist to not only Manolios, but to all who might be influenced to follow him in selfless devotion to the poor, specifically the refugees from a nearby village which has been pillaged by the Turks. The inevitable plot evolves, with the "sacrifice" of Manolios, but he doesn't accomplish his vision of peace and goodness in the village. There is even a "Pilate-like" figure in the Turkish Pasha, who is apparently the occupying force's representative in this pre-WWI community.<br /><br />A rather depressing commentary on the nature of the human <br />predicament--but the beauty of the landscape, the cycle of the seasons, and the unfolding of the characters made this a "good read", even for a non-believer like me.</span>
  <a data-text-id="14465161151565698131" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('1XgzB9aQkasFsQZJfsg2djTtQxn0KzFj4j0qdzrwXsZ+SScbhsimbLw0O2CKMztn39vlEK2VPe2ZSuJWMnjWvg==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer679273601328652058">Nikos Kazantzakis (Greek: <b>
  <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/17734780._________________' title='Νίκος Καζαντζάκης' rel='nofollow noopener'>Νίκος Καζαντζάκης</a>
</b>) was a prolific Greek writer, whose works include essays, novels, poems, tragedies, travel books, and translations of such classics as Dante's The Divine Comedy and J.W. von Goethe's Faust. <br/><br/>Like his hero, Odysseus, Kazantzakis lived most of his artistic life outside Greece-except for the years of World War II. "I am a mariner of Odysseus with heart o</span>
  <span id="freeText679273601328652058" style="display:none">Nikos Kazantzakis (Greek: <b>
  <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/17734780._________________" title="Νίκος Καζαντζάκης" rel="nofollow noopener">Νίκος Καζαντζάκης</a>
</b>) was a prolific Greek writer, whose works include essays, novels, poems, tragedies, travel books, and translations of such classics as Dante's The Divine Comedy and J.W. von Goethe's Faust. <br /><br />Like his hero, Odysseus, Kazantzakis lived most of his artistic life outside Greece-except for the years of World War II. "I am a mariner of Odysseus with heart of fire but with mind ruthless and clear," Kazantzakis wrote in TODA RABA (1934). <br /><br />Several of the author's novels deal with the history and culture of his own country, and the mystical relationship between man and God. In 1957 he lost the Nobel Prize by a single vote to the French writer Albert Camus.</span>
